      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Condoleezza_Rice_Visit_to_Georgia,_Press_Conference_with_Mikheil_Saakashvli.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Condoleezza_Rice_Visit_to_Georgia%2C_Press_Conference_with_Mikheil_Saakashvli.JPG/220px-Condoleezza_Rice_Visit_to_Georgia%2C_Press_Conference_with_Mikheil_Saakashvli.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Condoleezza_Rice_Visit_to_Georgia%2C_Press_Conference_with_Mikheil_Saakashvli.JPG/330px-Condoleezza_Rice_Visit_to_Georgia%2C_Press_Conference_with_Mikheil_Saakashvli.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Condoleezza_Rice_Visit_to_Georgia%2C_Press_Conference_with_Mikheil_Saakashvli.JPG/440px-Condoleezza_Rice_Visit_to_Georgia%2C_Press_Conference_with_Mikheil_Saakashvli.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Condoleezza_Rice_Visit_to_Georgia,_Press_Conference_with_Mikheil_Saakashvli.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Condoleezza_Rice" title="Condoleezza Rice">Condoleezza Rice</a> holding a joint press conference with Georgian president <a href="/wiki/Mikheil_Saakashvili" title="Mikheil Saakashvili">Mikheil Saakashvili</a> during the Russo–Georgian war
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/2008_Russo-Georgian_diplomatic_crisis" title="2008 Russo-Georgian diplomatic crisis">Tensions between Georgia and Russia began escalating</a> in April 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-shelling_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelling-87">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tall_order_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tall_order-88">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-counterproductive_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-counterproductive-89">[88]</a></sup> A bomb explosion on 1 August 2008 targeted a car transporting Georgian peacekeepers. South Ossetians were responsible for instigating this incident, which marked the opening of hostilities and injured five Georgian servicemen. In response,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> several South Ossetian militiamen were hit.<sup id="cite_ref-feud_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feud-91">[90]</a></sup> South Ossetian separatists began shelling Georgian villages on 1 August. These artillery attacks caused Georgian servicemen to return fire periodically since 1 August.<sup id="cite_ref-shelling_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelling-87">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-feud_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-feud-91">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Allison_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allison-93">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 30 September 2009, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>–sponsored <i>Independent International Fact-Finding Mission on the Conflict in Georgia</i> stated that, while preceded by months of mutual provocations, "open hostilities began with a large-scale Georgian military operation against the town of Tskhinvali and the surrounding areas, launched in the night of 7 to 8 August 2008."<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>At around 19:00 on 7 August 2008, Georgian president Mikheil Saakashvili announced a unilateral <a href="/wiki/Ceasefire" title="Ceasefire">ceasefire</a> and called for peace talks.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> However, escalating assaults against Georgian villages (located in the South Ossetian conflict zone) were soon matched with gunfire from Georgian troops,<sup id="cite_ref-Jamestown_Goals_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jamestown_Goals-98">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> who then proceeded to move in the direction of the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Self-proclaimed" title="Self-proclaimed">self-proclaimed</a> Republic of South Ossetia (<a href="/wiki/Tskhinvali" title="Tskhinvali">Tskhinvali</a>) on the night of 8 August, reaching its centre in the morning of 8 August.<sup id="cite_ref-washingtonpost.com_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost.com-100">[99]</a></sup> One Georgian diplomat told Russian newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Kommersant" title="Kommersant">Kommersant</a></i> on 8 August that by taking control of Tskhinvali, <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi" title="Tbilisi">Tbilisi</a> wanted to demonstrate that Georgia would not tolerate the killing of Georgian citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-rmd_source_1_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rmd_source_1-101">[100]</a></sup> According to Russian military expert <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Felgenhauer" title="Pavel Felgenhauer">Pavel Felgenhauer</a>, the Ossetian provocation was aimed at triggering the Georgian response, which was needed as a <a href="/wiki/Pretext" title="Pretext">pretext</a> for premeditated Russian military invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> According to Georgian intelligence,<sup id="cite_ref-NYTFreshev_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTFreshev-103">[102]</a></sup> and several Russian media reports, parts of the regular (non-peacekeeping) Russian Army had already moved to South Ossetian territory through the <a href="/wiki/Roki_Tunnel" title="Roki Tunnel">Roki Tunnel</a> before the Georgian military action.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Russian_Military_Bases_in_Abkhazia_(2011-2016_Data).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Russian_Military_Bases_in_Abkhazia_%282011-2016_Data%29.svg/220px-Russian_Military_Bases_in_Abkhazia_%282011-2016_Data%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Russian_Military_Bases_in_Abkhazia_%282011-2016_Data%29.svg/330px-Russian_Military_Bases_in_Abkhazia_%282011-2016_Data%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Russian_Military_Bases_in_Abkhazia_%282011-2016_Data%29.svg/440px-Russian_Military_Bases_in_Abkhazia_%282011-2016_Data%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="735"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Russian_Military_Bases_in_Abkhazia_(2011-2016_Data).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Russian Military Bases in Abkhazia as of 2016<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Russia accused Georgia of "aggression against South Ossetia",<sup id="cite_ref-roudik_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roudik-105">[104]</a></sup> and launched a large-scale land, air and sea invasion of Georgia with the pretext of "<a href="/wiki/Peace_enforcement" title="Peace enforcement">peace enforcement</a>" operation on 8 August 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Allison_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allison-93">[92]</a></sup> Russian <a href="/wiki/Airstrike" title="Airstrike">airstrikes</a> against targets within Georgia were also launched.<sup id="cite_ref-radar_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radar-106">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Abkhazia" class="mw-redirect" title="Military of Abkhazia">Abkhaz forces</a> opened a second front on 9 August by <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Kodori_Valley" title="Battle of the Kodori Valley">attacking the Kodori Gorge</a>, held by Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> Tskhinvali was seized by the Russian military by 10 August.<sup id="cite_ref-radar_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radar-106">[105]</a></sup> Russian forces occupied the Georgian cities of <a href="/wiki/Zugdidi" title="Zugdidi">Zugdidi</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-new_front_deeper_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-new_front_deeper-108">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Senaki" title="Senaki">Senaki</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-gori_central_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gori_central-109">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Poti" title="Poti">Poti</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Russia_NYTimes_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russia_NYTimes-110">[109]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Gori,_Georgia" title="Gori, Georgia">Gori</a> (the last one after the ceasefire agreement was negotiated).<sup id="cite_ref-gori_major_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gori_major-111">[110]</a></sup> Russian <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea_Fleet" title="Black Sea Fleet">Black Sea Fleet</a> blockaded the Georgian coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Allison_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allison-93">[92]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>South Ossetians conducted a campaign of <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing_of_Georgians_in_South_Ossetia" title="Ethnic cleansing of Georgians in South Ossetia">ethnic cleansing against Georgians in South Ossetia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Fact-finding_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fact-finding-112">[111]</a></sup> destroying Georgian villages around Tskhinvali after the war had ended.<sup id="cite_ref-Damage_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Damage-113">[112]</a></sup> The war displaced 192,000 people,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> and while many were able to return to their homes after the war, a year later around 30,000 ethnic Georgians remained displaced.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> In an interview published in <i>Kommersant</i>, South Ossetian leader <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Kokoity" title="Eduard Kokoity">Eduard Kokoity</a> said he would not allow Georgians to return.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">President of France</a> <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> negotiated a ceasefire agreement on 12 August 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> On 17 August, Russian president <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Medvedev" title="Dmitry Medvedev">Dmitry Medvedev</a> announced that Russian forces would begin to pull out of Georgia the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-http_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-http-119">[118]</a></sup> Russia recognised Abkhazia and South Ossetia as separate republics on 26 August.<sup id="cite_ref-kremlin_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kremlin-120">[119]</a></sup> In response to Russia's recognition, the Georgian government severed diplomatic relations with Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-break_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-break-121">[120]</a></sup> Russian forces left the buffer areas bordering <a href="/wiki/Abkhazia" title="Abkhazia">Abkhazia</a> and South Ossetia on 8 October, and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Monitoring_Mission" class="mw-redirect" title="European Union Monitoring Mission">European Union Monitoring Mission</a> in Georgia was dispatched to the buffer areas.<sup id="cite_ref-en.rian_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-en.rian-122">[121]</a></sup> Since the war, Georgia has maintained that Abkhazia and South Ossetia are <a href="/wiki/Occupied_territories_of_Georgia" title="Occupied territories of Georgia">occupied Georgian territories</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-parliament.ge_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parliament.ge-123">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Georgia_(country)" title="Politics of Georgia (country)">Politics of Georgia (country)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Zourabichvili_at_Official_Meeting.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Zourabichvili_at_Official_Meeting.jpg/220px-Zourabichvili_at_Official_Meeting.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Zourabichvili_at_Official_Meeting.jpg/330px-Zourabichvili_at_Official_Meeting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Zourabichvili_at_Official_Meeting.jpg/440px-Zourabichvili_at_Official_Meeting.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4932" data-file-height="3269"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Zourabichvili_at_Official_Meeting.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Salome_Zourabichvili" title="Salome Zourabichvili">Salome Zourabichvili</a>, the first woman elected as president of Georgia
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Orbeliani_Palace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Orbeliani_Palace.jpg/220px-Orbeliani_Palace.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Orbeliani_Palace.jpg/330px-Orbeliani_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Orbeliani_Palace.jpg/440px-Orbeliani_Palace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5600" data-file-height="3749"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Orbeliani_Palace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Presidential residence at the Orbeliani Palace in <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi" title="Tbilisi">Tbilisi</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Georgia is a <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary">parliamentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Georgia" title="President of Georgia">President</a> as the head of state, and Prime Minister as the head of government. The executive branch of power is made up of the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Georgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Cabinet of Georgia">Cabinet of Georgia</a>. The Cabinet is composed of ministers, headed by the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Georgia" title="Prime Minister of Georgia">Prime Minister</a>, and appointed by the President. <a href="/wiki/Salome_Zurabishvili" class="mw-redirect" title="Salome Zurabishvili">Salome Zurabishvili</a> is the current President of Georgia after winning 59.52% of the vote in the <a href="/wiki/Georgian_presidential_election,_2018" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgian presidential election, 2018">2018 Georgian presidential election</a>. Since 2019, <a href="/wiki/Giorgi_Gakharia" title="Giorgi Gakharia">Giorgi Gakharia</a> has been the Prime Minister of Georgia. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">Legislative</a> authority is vested in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Georgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Georgia">Parliament of Georgia</a>. It is unicameral and has 150 members, known as deputies, of whom 73 are elected by plurality to represent single-member districts, and 77 are chosen to represent parties by proportional representation. Members of parliament are elected for four-year terms. On 26 May 2012, Saakashvili inaugurated a new Parliament building in the western city of <a href="/wiki/Kutaisi" title="Kutaisi">Kutaisi</a>, in an effort to decentralise power and shift some political control closer to Abkhazia.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Georgian_parliamentary_election,_2012" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgian parliamentary election, 2012">elections in October 2012</a> resulted in the victory for the opposition "<a href="/wiki/Georgian_Dream" title="Georgian Dream">Georgian Dream – Democratic Georgia</a>" <a href="/wiki/Coalition#Politics_and_government" title="Coalition">coalition</a>, which President Saakashvili acknowledged on the following day.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Different opinions exist regarding the degree of political freedom in Georgia. Saakashvili believed in 2008 that the country is "on the road to becoming a European democracy."<sup id="cite_ref-misha_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-misha-127">[126]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a> lists Georgia as a partly free country.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In preparation for 2012 parliamentary elections, Parliament adopted a new electoral code on 27 December 2011 that incorporated many recommendations from non-governmental organizations (NGOs) and the Venice Commission. However, the new code failed to address the Venice Commission's primary recommendation to strengthen the equality of the vote by reconstituting single-mandate election districts to be comparable in size. On 28 December, Parliament amended the Law on Political Unions to regulate campaign and political party financing. Local and international observers raised concerns about several amendments, including the vagueness of the criteria for determining political bribery and which individuals and organizations would be subject to the law. As of March&nbsp;2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georgia_(country)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Parliament was discussing further amendments to address these concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Georgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign relations of Georgia">Foreign relations of Georgia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nato_poster_tbilisi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Nato_poster_tbilisi.jpg/170px-Nato_poster_tbilisi.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Nato_poster_tbilisi.jpg/255px-Nato_poster_tbilisi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Nato_poster_tbilisi.jpg/340px-Nato_poster_tbilisi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1854" data-file-height="2880"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nato_poster_tbilisi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Pro-<a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> poster in <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi" title="Tbilisi">Tbilisi</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Georgia maintains good relations with its direct neighbours (Armenia, Azerbaijan, and Turkey) and is a member of the United Nations, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Black_Sea_Economic_Cooperation" title="Organization of the Black Sea Economic Cooperation">Organization of the Black Sea Economic Cooperation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Security_and_Cooperation_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization for Security and Cooperation in Europe">Organization for Security and Cooperation in Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Democratic_Choice" title="Community of Democratic Choice">Community of Democratic Choice</a>, the <a href="/wiki/GUAM_Organization_for_Democracy_and_Economic_Development" title="GUAM Organization for Democracy and Economic Development">GUAM Organization for Democracy and Economic Development</a>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Bank_for_Reconstruction_and_Development" title="European Bank for Reconstruction and Development">European Bank for Reconstruction and Development</a><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Development_Bank" title="Asian Development Bank">Asian Development Bank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> Georgia also maintains political, economic, and military relations with France,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> Germany,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> Turkey,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup> and many other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The growing U.S. and European Union influence in Georgia, notably through proposed EU and NATO membership, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Train_and_Equip_Program" title="Georgia Train and Equip Program">Train and Equip</a> military assistance program, and the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Baku%E2%80%93Tbilisi%E2%80%93Ceyhan_pipeline" title="Baku–Tbilisi–Ceyhan pipeline">Baku–Tbilisi–Ceyhan pipeline</a> have frequently strained Tbilisi's relations with Moscow. Georgia's decision to boost its presence in the coalition forces in Iraq was an important initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Georgia is currently working to become a full member of <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization">NATO</a>. In August 2004, the Individual Partnership Action Plan of Georgia was submitted officially to NATO. On 29 October 2004, the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Council" title="North Atlantic Council">North Atlantic Council</a> of NATO approved the <a href="/wiki/NATO#International_Partnership_Action_Plans" title="NATO">Individual Partnership Action Plan</a> (IPAP) of Georgia, and Georgia moved on to the second stage of Euro–Atlantic Integration. In 2005, the agreement on the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Partnership_for_Peace" title="Partnership for Peace">Partnership for Peace</a> (PfP) liaison officer between Georgia and NATO came into force, whereby a liaison officer for the South Caucasus was assigned to Georgia. On 2 March 2005, the agreement was signed on the provision of the host nation support to and transit of NATO forces and NATO personnel. On 6–9 March 2006, the IPAP implementation interim assessment team arrived in Tbilisi. On 13 April 2006, the discussion of the assessment report on implementation of the Individual Partnership Action Plan was held at NATO Headquarters, within 26+1 format.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> The majority of Georgians and politicians in Georgia support the push for NATO membership.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2011, <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Council" title="North Atlantic Council">North Atlantic Council</a> designated Georgia as an "aspirant country".<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> Since 2014, Georgia–NATO relations are guided by the Substantial NATO–Georgia Package (SNGP), which includes NATO–Georgia Joint Training and Evaluation Centre and facilitation of multi-national and regional military drills.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In September 2019, Russian Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Lavrov" title="Sergey Lavrov">Sergey Lavrov</a> said that "NATO approaching our borders is a threat to Russia."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> He was quoted as saying that if <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> accepts Georgian membership with the article on collective defense covering only Tbilisi-administered territory (i.e., excluding the Georgian territories <a href="/wiki/Abkhazia" title="Abkhazia">Abkhazia</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Ossetia" title="South Ossetia">South Ossetia</a>, both of which are currently Russian-supported <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_limited_recognition" title="List of states with limited recognition">unrecognized breakaway republics</a>), "we will not start a war, but such conduct will undermine our relations with NATO and with countries who are eager to enter the alliance."<sup id="cite_ref-LavrovNATO2019_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LavrovNATO2019-148">[147]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> became the first sitting U.S. president to visit the country.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> The street leading to <a href="/wiki/Tbilisi_International_Airport" title="Tbilisi International Airport">Tbilisi International Airport</a> has since been dubbed George W. Bush Avenue.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> On 2 October 2006, Georgia and the European Union signed a joint statement on the agreed text of the Georgia–European Union Action Plan within the <a href="/wiki/European_Neighbourhood_Policy" title="European Neighbourhood Policy">European Neighbourhood Policy</a> (ENP). The Action Plan was formally approved at the EU–Georgia Cooperation Council session on 14 November 2006, in <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup> In June 2014, the EU and Georgia signed an <a href="/wiki/Association_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Association Agreement">Association Agreement</a>, which entered into force on 1 July 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup> On 13 December 2016, <a href="/wiki/EU" class="mw-redirect" title="EU">EU</a> and Georgia reached the agreement on visa liberalisation for Georgian citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup> On 27 February 2017, the Council adopted a regulation on visa liberalisation for Georgians travelling to the EU for a period of stay of 90 days in any 180-day period.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Defense_Forces_of_Georgia" title="Defense Forces of Georgia">Defense Forces of Georgia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Role_of_Georgia_in_the_War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%9314)" class="mw-redirect" title="Role of Georgia in the War in Afghanistan (2001–14)">Role of Georgia in the War in Afghanistan (2001–14)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:APC_-_Didgori-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/APC_-_Didgori-2.jpg/220px-APC_-_Didgori-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/APC_-_Didgori-2.jpg/330px-APC_-_Didgori-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/APC_-_Didgori-2.jpg/440px-APC_-_Didgori-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:APC_-_Didgori-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Georgian built <a href="/wiki/Didgori-2" title="Didgori-2">Didgori-2</a> during a parade in 2011
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Georgia's military is organized into <a href="/wiki/Georgian_Land_Forces" title="Georgian Land Forces">land</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgian_Air_Force" title="Georgian Air Force">air</a> forces. They are collectively known as the Georgian Armed Forces (GAF).<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Factbook_Georgia_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Factbook_Georgia-155">[154]</a></sup> The mission and functions of the GAF are based on the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Georgia_(country)" title="Constitution of Georgia (country)">Constitution of Georgia</a>, Georgia's Law on Defense and National Military Strategy, and international agreements to which Georgia is signatory.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The military budget of Georgia for 2017 is 748<a href="/wiki/Georgian_lari" title="Georgian lari">₾</a> million, by 78<a href="/wiki/Georgian_lari" title="Georgian lari">₾</a> million more than in 2016. The biggest part, 62.5% of the military budget is allocated for maintaining armored forces readiness and potency development.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> After its independence from the Soviet Union, Georgia began to develop its own <a href="/wiki/Military_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Military industry">military industry</a>. The first exhibition of products made by <a href="/wiki/STC_Delta" title="STC Delta">STC Delta</a> was in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> STC Delta now produces a variety of <a href="/wiki/STC_Delta#Military_and_civilian_production" title="STC Delta">military equipment</a>, including armored vehicles, artillery systems, aviation systems, personal protection equipment, and small arms.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During later periods of the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a> Georgia had up to 2,000 soldiers serving in the <a href="/wiki/Multi-National_Force_%E2%80%93_Iraq" title="Multi-National Force – Iraq">Multi-National Force</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup> Georgia also participated in the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>-led <a href="/wiki/International_Security_Assistance_Force" title="International Security Assistance Force">International Security Assistance Force</a> in <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Afghanistan</a>. With 1,560 troops in 2013, it was at that time the largest non-<a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a><sup id="cite_ref-civil1_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-civil1-160">[159]</a></sup> and the largest <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> troop contributor. Over 11,000 Georgian soldiers have been rotated through Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Georgia_(country)&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 31 Georgian servicemen <a href="/wiki/Coalition_casualties_in_Afghanistan" title="Coalition casualties in Afghanistan">have died in Afghanistan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> most during the <a href="/wiki/Helmand_province_campaign" title="Helmand province campaign">Helmand campaign</a>, and 435 were wounded, including 35 amputees.<sup id="cite_ref-civil2_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-civil2-165">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_enforcement">Law enforcement</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_in_Georgia_(country)" title="Law enforcement in Georgia (country)">Law enforcement in Georgia (country)</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Police_car_in_Tbilisi_(69).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Police_car_in_Tbilisi_%2869%29.jpg/220px-Police_car_in_Tbilisi_%2869%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Police_car_in_Tbilisi_%2869%29.jpg/330px-Police_car_in_Tbilisi_%2869%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Police_car_in_Tbilisi_%2869%29.jpg/440px-Police_car_in_Tbilisi_%2869%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Police_car_in_Tbilisi_(69).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A <a href="/wiki/Ford_Taurus_(sixth_generation)#Police_Interceptor_Sedan" title="Ford Taurus (sixth generation)">Ford Taurus Police Interceptor</a> operated by the Georgian Patrol Police.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In Georgia, law enforcement is conducted and provided for by the Ministry of Internal Affairs of Georgia. In recent years, the Patrol Police Department of the Ministry of Internal Affairs of Georgia has undergone a radical transformation, with the police having now absorbed a great many duties previously performed by dedicated independent government agencies. New duties performed by the police include border security and customs functions and contracted security provision; the latter function is performed by the dedicated 'security police'.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In 2005, President <a href="/wiki/Mikheil_Saakashvili" title="Mikheil Saakashvili">Mikheil Saakashvili</a> fired the entire traffic police force (numbering around 30,000 police officers) of the Georgian National Police due to corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-mcclatchydc.com_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcclatchydc.com-167">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup> A new force was then subsequently built around new recruits.<sup id="cite_ref-mcclatchydc.com_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcclatchydc.com-167">[166]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/US_State_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="US State Department">US State Department</a>'s Bureau of International Narcotics and Law-Enforcement Affairs has provided assistance to the training efforts and continues to act in an advisory capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The new <i>Patruli</i> force was first introduced in the summer of 2005 to replace the traffic police, a force which was accused of widespread corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> The police introduced an 022 (currently 112) <a href="/wiki/Emergency_telephone_number" title="Emergency telephone number">emergency dispatch</a> service in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Georgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Human rights in Georgia">Human rights in Georgia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Human rights in Georgia are guaranteed by the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Georgia_(country)" title="Constitution of Georgia (country)">country's constitution</a>. There is an independent human rights <i>public defender</i> elected by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Georgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliament of Georgia">Parliament of Georgia</a> to ensure such rights are enforced.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> Georgia has ratified the <a href="/wiki/Framework_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_National_Minorities" title="Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities">Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities</a> in 2005. NGO "Tolerance", in its alternative report about its implementation, speaks of a rapid decrease in the number of Azerbaijani schools and cases of appointing headmasters to Azerbaijani schools who do not speak the <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The government came under criticism for its alleged use of excessive force on 26 May 2011 when it dispersed <a href="/wiki/2011_Georgian_protests" title="2011 Georgian protests">protesters</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Nino_Burjanadze" title="Nino Burjanadze">Nino Burjanadze</a>, among others, with tear gas and rubber bullets after they refused to clear <a href="/wiki/Rustaveli_avenue" class="mw-redirect" title="Rustaveli avenue">Rustaveli avenue</a> for an independence day parade despite the expiration of their demonstration permit and despite being offered to choose an alternative venue.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ABC-AP_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-AP-177">[176]</a></sup> While human rights activists maintained that the protests were peaceful, the government pointed out that many protesters were masked and armed with heavy sticks and molotov cocktails.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> Georgian opposition leader Nino Burjanadze said the accusations of planning a coup were baseless, and that the protesters' actions were legitimate.<sup id="cite_ref-ABC-AP_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-AP-177">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Georgia_(country)" title="Administrative divisions of Georgia (country)">Administrative divisions of Georgia (country)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abkhazia" title="Abkhazia">Abkhazia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Adjara" title="Adjara">Adjara</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Occupied_territories_of_Georgia" title="Occupied territories of Georgia">Occupied territories of Georgia</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Georgia_high_detail_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Georgia_high_detail_map.png/220px-Georgia_high_detail_map.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Georgia_high_detail_map.png/330px-Georgia_high_detail_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Georgia_high_detail_map.png/440px-Georgia_high_detail_map.png 2x" data-file-width="2529" data-file-height="1872"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Georgia_high_detail_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Map of Georgia highlighting the disputed territories of <a href="/wiki/Abkhazia" title="Abkhazia">Abkhazia</a> and Tskhinvali Region (<i><a href="/wiki/South_Ossetia" title="South Ossetia">South Ossetia</a></i>), both of which are outside the control of the central government of Georgia
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Georgia is divided into 9 regions, 1 city, and 2 autonomous republics.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_Factbook_Georgia_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA_Factbook_Georgia-155">[154]</a></sup> These in turn are subdivided into 67 <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Georgia_(country)" class="mw-redirect" title="Districts of Georgia (country)">districts</a> and 12 self-governing cities.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Georgia contains two official autonomous regions, of which one has declared independence. Officially autonomous within Georgia,<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> the de facto independent region of <a href="/wiki/Abkhazia" title="Abkhazia">Abkhazia</a> declared independence in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> In addition, another territory not officially autonomous has also declared independence. <a href="/wiki/South_Ossetia" title="South Ossetia">South Ossetia</a> is officially known by Georgia as the Tskinvali region, as it views "South Ossetia" as implying political bonds with Russian <a href="/wiki/North_Ossetia" class="mw-redirect" title="North Ossetia">North Ossetia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_South_Ossetia_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_South_Ossetia-183">[182]</a></sup> It was called <a href="/wiki/South_Ossetian_Autonomous_Oblast" title="South Ossetian Autonomous Oblast">South Ossetian Autonomous Oblast</a> when Georgia was part of Soviet Union. Its autonomous status was revoked in 1990. De facto separate since Georgian independence, offers were made to give South Ossetia autonomy again, but in 2006 an unrecognised referendum in the area resulted in a vote for independence.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_South_Ossetia_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_South_Ossetia-183">[182]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In both Abkhazia and South Ossetia large numbers of people had been given Russian passports, some through a process of forced <a href="/wiki/Passportization" title="Passportization">passportization</a> by Russian authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup> This was used as a justification for Russian invasion of Georgia during the 2008 South Ossetia war after which Russia recognised the region's independence.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> Georgia considers the regions as occupied by Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-parliament.ge_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parliament.ge-123">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup> The two republics gained <a href="/wiki/International_recognition_of_Abkhazia_and_South_Ossetia" title="International recognition of Abkhazia and South Ossetia">limited international recognition</a> after the 2008 Russo-Georgian War. Most countries consider the regions to be <a href="/wiki/Occupied_territories_of_Georgia" title="Occupied territories of Georgia">Georgian territory under Russian occupation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-smr_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smr-187">[186]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Adjara" title="Adjara">Adjara</a> under local strongman <a href="/wiki/Aslan_Abashidze" title="Aslan Abashidze">Aslan Abashidze</a> maintained close ties with Russia and allowed a Russian military base to be maintained in Batumi. Upon the election of Mikheil Saakashvili in 2004 tensions rose between Abashidze and the Georgian government, leading to demonstrations in Adjara and the resignation and flight of Abashidze. The region retains autonomy, and as a sign of Ajaria's reconnection with the central Georgian government, the Georgian Constitutional Court was moved from T'bilisi to Batumi.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> </p>

          </div> </td>
